  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_a2373d90-19ab-4716-a708-82097d5fc3a9'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer11585207996633939442"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38926465.The_Body' title='The Body by Stephen King' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Body</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3389.Stephen_King' title='Stephen King' rel='nofollow noopener'>Stephen King</a><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1604923307ra/30354640.gif' width='480' height='274' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>Such a wonderful story that we can relate to the characters' lives and their interaction with each other. Stephen King did a wonderful job of writing this book about characters in a way that made them seem completely genuine and authentic. The emotions that the author evoked were deep and the ending will have you pondering for a certain long time.<br/><i>
  <blockquote>Love isn’t soft, like those poets say. Love has teeth which bite and the wounds never close.</blockquote>
</i><br/><i>Charming book.</i></span>
  <span id="freeText11585207996633939442" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38926465.The_Body" title="The Body by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Body</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3389.Stephen_King" title="Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Stephen King</a><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1604923307ra/30354640.gif" width="480" height="274" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Such a wonderful story that we can relate to the characters' lives and their interaction with each other.     <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_101526711" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101526711" />
    <a title="Nataliya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya"><img alt="Nataliya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605995014p2/3672777.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101526711?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nataliya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nataliya" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya">Nataliya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone who's looking for a well-written character-based story</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=i-also-saw-the-film">i-also-saw-the-film</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=stephen-king">stephen-king</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=excellent-reads">excellent-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2012-reads">2012-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101526711" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9353768629793209205"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334647i/693944._SX540_.jpg' width='400' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>For all of those who keep insisting that Stephen King is a literary equivalent of Big Mac and fries, writing in the comfortable confines of the frequently-despised 'genre' - please take a look at <i>The Body: The Fall from Innocence</i>, which is much more familiar to public in the quite faithful adaptation by Rob Reiner - <i>'Stand by Me'.</i> <br/><br/>It's not King's trademark horror; it is actually free of the constraints of any so-called 'genre'. <b>It is a coming-of-age character-study novella set in 1960 Maine wher</b></span>
  <span id="freeText9353768629793209205" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334647i/693944._SX540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>For all of those who keep insisting that Stephen King is a literary equivalent of Big Mac and fries, writing in the comfortable confines of the frequently-despised 'genre' - please take a look at <i>The Body: The Fall from Innocence</i>, which is much more familiar to public in the quite faithful adaptation by Rob Reiner - <i>'Stand by Me'.</i> <br><br>It's not King's trademark horror; it is actually free of the constraints of any so-called 'genre'. <b>It is a coming-of-age character-study novella set in 1960 Maine where monsters are not hiding behind bushes but instead live in the hearts of people</b> - the setting and themes at which King excels.<br><br>******<img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334647i/693945.jpg" width="300" class="gr-hostedUserImg">******<br><br>This is a story of four boys on the brink of adolescence; <b>
  <i>the last moments of childhood told with occasional almost Bradbury-esque nostalgia but with the rose-tinted glasses mercilessly torn off.</i>
</b> The blue-collar childhood in a small Maine town in 1960 is not a place of magic and wonder - these boys are no strangers to abandonment and abuse and prejudice. <br><br>Hot-tempered and volatile <b>Teddy Duchamp</b> has been physically mutilated by his mentally ill father whom he still worships. Childish and not-too-bright <b>Vern Tessio</b> lives in fear of his brother. <b>Gordie Lachance</b>, whose adult writer self is telling us this story, is little but a stranger to his parents who never got over the death of his older brother. Smart and tough <b>Chris Chambers</b>, a kid from a family that supplies Castle Rock with alcoholics and juvenile delinquents, is being seriously abused by his father and is seen as a worthless and even dangerous person because of his family.<blockquote>"<i>Chris didn't talk much about his dad, but we all knew he hated him like poison. Chris was marked up every two weeks or so, bruises on his cheeks and neck or one eye swelled up and as colorful as a sunset, and once he came to school with a big clumsy bandage on the back of his head. Other times he never got to school at all. His mom would call him in sick because he was too lamed up to come in. Chris was smart, really smart, but he played truant a lot, and Mr. Halliburton, the town truant officer, was always showing up at Chris's house, driving his old black Chevrolet with the NO RIDERS sticker in the corner of the windshield. If Chris was being truant and Bertie (as we called him - always behind his back, of course) caught him, he would haul him back to school and see that Chris got detention for a week. But if Bertie found out that Chris was home because his father had beaten the shit out of him, Bertie just went away and didn't say boo to a cuckoo bird. It never occurred to me to question this set of priorities until about twenty years later.</i>"</blockquote><b>But childhood, even though not at all sheltered, still gives them something of a shield against the world</b> - that sense of invulnerability that only the young children have, the love for adventure, and the protection of sincere and lighthearted friendship.<blockquote>"<i>Everything was there and around us. We knew exactly who we were and exactly where we were going. It was grand.</i>"</blockquote> <br>*****<img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334647i/693946.jpg" width="250" class="gr-hostedUserImg">*****<br><br><b>But we meet them right at the time when they are about to leave the protection of childhood behind them</b>, when in the miserably hot summer of 1960 they set out on a trip to find a body of a boy who disappeared in the woods - a trip that makes at least two of them go through quite significant emotional turmoil and reevaluate their priorities and see the strengthening of one friendship while the others fall apart as the realization sets in that there is more to friendship than just fun and leisure. This is a trip that uncovers both the steel and the vulnerability in the characters of Chris and Gordie, and shoves them from the haven of childhood into the world where things take work and sacrifice and pain, the world that is often cruel and cynical and unavoidable.<blockquote>"<i>But he said: "Your friends drag you down, Gordie. Don't you know that? [...] Your friends do. They're like drowning guys that are holding onto your legs. You can't save them. You can only drown with them.</i>"</blockquote>**<img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334647i/693947.jpg" width="300" class="gr-hostedUserImg">**<br><br>This is a scary realization when you are young - that your friends are not good for you. I remember getting that feeling at around twelve, the age the boys in this book are, and I remember how unsettling that realization was. At that time it feels like friendships are forever, and that things that connect you to other people are there to stay - and realizing how easy and even necessary it can be to break those bonds is quite unsettling. <blockquote>"<i>You always know the truth, because when you cut yourself or someone else with it, there's always a bloody show.</i>"</blockquote>***<img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334647i/693948.jpg" width="350" class="gr-hostedUserImg">***<br><br>And some of this is present here - but on the other hand we are also treated to the strengthening of the true friendship between Gordie and Chris. <b>Gordie, a kid who is emotionally neglected by his family, acutely feels the sincerity and kindness that Chris brings into the world, despite his 'tough' origins</b> - Chris, the center of this ragtag group, is grown up beyond his years, and has some hard-earned wisdom for his twelve years of age, sprinkled with a bit of pain and bitterness but grounded in common sense.<blockquote>"<i>But it was only survival. We were clinging to each other in deep water. I've explained about Chris, I think; my reasons for clinging to him were less definable. His desire to get away from Castle Rock and out of the mill's shadow seemed to me to be my best part, and I could not just leave him to sink or swim on his own. If he had drowned, that part of me would have drowned with him, I think.</i>"</blockquote>*****<img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334647i/693949.jpg" width="300" class="gr-hostedUserImg">*****<br><br>I love the narrative voice of this story - the narration by a young but accomplished writer Gordon Lachance, bringing the perspective that the few decades that have passed since that summer of 1960 have given him - but yet conveying the feelings and the attitudes of a twelve-year-old boy who feels both resentment and love and experiences profound beauty and the low of human ugliness. There are lyrical parts and trademark-King unflinching gory parts, and social commentary without the slightest sugar-coating. The story is peppered in places with the stories written by older Gordon and full of reflections of the adult man reflecting on the important and defining experience of the end of his childhood. <blockquote>"<i>The most important things are hardest to say, because words diminish them.</i>"</blockquote> It is a fascinating, engrossing read, the one that is well worth several hours of your time, even if you have never been a fan of King. <b>5 stars and highly recommend!</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="9353768629793209205" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3278093257" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3278093257" />
    <a title="Peter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10477405-peter"><img alt="Peter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340652114p2/10477405.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3278093257?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter" href="/user/show/10477405-peter">Peter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10477405-peter?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3278093257" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer712764473008145180">Interesting novella showing the lives of four young boys growing up in the early 60s. Teddy, Chris, Vern and Gordon ("Gordie", the narrator who later becomes an author). You'll find typical small town life (Castle Rock), references to other King works (Cujo) and see the boys gradually loose their innocence and childhood. They are looking for the body of Ray Brower (of course I had to think about Henry Bowers from It). You'll read a lot about violent fathers, how to get acquainted with women and </span>
  <span id="freeText712764473008145180" style="display:none">Interesting novella showing the lives of four young boys growing up in the early 60s. Teddy, Chris, Vern and Gordon ("Gordie", the narrator who later becomes an author). You'll find typical small town life (Castle Rock), references to other King works (Cujo) and see the boys gradually loose their innocence and childhood. They are looking for the body of Ray Brower (of course I had to think about Henry Bowers from It). You'll read a lot about violent fathers, how to get acquainted with women and bed them, bullies, what books formed the kids' minds and which direction their lives took when getting older. Brilliant coming of age tale. Melancholic and sad in some parts but vibrant and full of life. Absolutely recommended!</span>
  <a data-text-id="712764473008145180" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2609317978" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2609317978" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5903843-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603562140p2/5903843.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2609317978?book_show_action=true">Nov 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/5903843-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781982103538" href="/book/show/38926465-the-body">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2609317978" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11958344856417395015">Stephen King’s wonderful 1982 novella, which was transformed into the classic 1986 film, <i>Stand By Me</i>, four young boys to come of age over a weekend together. Set in 1960, the story takes place in the small town of Castle Rock, Maine, where twelve-year-old Gordie Lachance and his three friends are ready to set out to substantiate the rumours that the body of a missing boy has turned up near the next town. As the boys to begin their summer trek, they must come together to face winding train tracks</span>
  <span id="freeText11958344856417395015" style="display:none">Stephen King’s wonderful 1982 novella, which was transformed into the classic 1986 film, <i>Stand By Me</i>, four young boys to come of age over a weekend together. Set in 1960, the story takes place in the small town of Castle Rock, Maine, where twelve-year-old Gordie Lachance and his three friends are ready to set out to substantiate the rumours that the body of a missing boy has turned up near the next town. As the boys to begin their summer trek, they must come together to face winding train tracks, a brief dip in an interesting water hole, and a great deal of self-discovery. In a story that seeks to explore the innermost thoughts and feelings of these four, the reader can see that emotions run deep and that the ‘tough guy’ exteriors are only a pre-teen facade. King pulls the reader in from the outset in this well-paced piece, which shows just how amazing youth can be, when tempered with a little sobering maturity. Recommended for those who like King and his various writing styles. No need to be wary, for there is little gore, but enough language that some readers may want to look elsewhere.<br /><br />I always enjoy Stephen King pieces, as they keep me wondering where things will go in his circuitous writing style. There was a strict ban on my reading his novels when I was younger, for reasons I am not entirely sure I remember. My adult years have been spent catching up and I have come to see that King can be a little intense, but he has a great deal I thoroughly enjoy. King offers up a lighter novella here, allowing his characters to develop nicely without the excessive gore. Gordie Lachance is both the presumptive protagonist and the ‘author’ of this story, a flashback piece penned when he was much older. Lachance explores some of the sentiments of his own childhood, as well as honing his skills as a writer. Gordie offers up much development as it relates to his friends, giving the reader a more comprehensive approach to those who populate the story. Through a series of events that weave together into the larger story, King allows his characters to mature through their learning experiences. Keeping the reader engaged throughout this quick read, King shows just how strong his writing can be, close to four decades later. <br /><br />Kudos, Mr. King, for another wonderful piece of writing. I am happy to have stumbled upon this one and will admit that I have not seen <i>Stand By Me</i> in its entirety, which will soon change.<br /><br />Like/hate the review? An ever-growing collection of others appears at: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://pecheyponderings.wordpress.com/">http://pecheyponderings.wordpress.com/</a><br /><br />A Book for All Seasons, a different sort of Book Challenge: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/248185-a-book-for-all-seasons">https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="11958344856417395015" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

There’s a good chance he’ll choose to be contrary simply to hurt my feelings since even the most decent teenager is still pretty horrible. The good news is I was able to pull a double-whammy and make the youngest listen too on the way to and from his baseball tournament this weekend. He arrived a little late to the party when Gordy and the boys were getting ready to meet Milo Pressman and the notorious “Chopper” and was on the edge of his seat during the train dodge. He completely blew me away when he complained as I hit strategically hit pause at a certain point in the story so we could hear it in full the next morning. And what a morning we had! A total barf-o-rama full of cackling and full-blown guffaws. <br><br><a href="https://lunapic.com" rel="nofollow noopener">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer667414785" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer15122196476138088919">I really liked the movie <b>Stand By Me</b> so I knew going in to expect a great read, but what I found was so much more.<p><b>The Body</b> is a timeless coming-of-age story. Set in late summer of 1960's Maine, four twelve-year-old buddies, all with strangely abusive and dysfunctional families, take a longer than anticipated walking trip in hopes of seeing a dead body rumored to be hit by a train. As their many harrowing adventures and touching personal stories unfold, they run into <i>big</i> trouble with some older d</p></span>
  <span id="freeText15122196476138088919" style="display:none">I really liked the movie <b>Stand By Me</b> so I knew going in to expect a great read, but what I found was so much more.<p><b>The Body</b> is a timeless coming-of-age story. Set in late summer of 1960's Maine, four twelve-year-old buddies, all with strangely abusive and dysfunctional families, take a longer than anticipated walking trip in hopes of seeing a dead body rumored to be hit by a train. As their many harrowing adventures and touching personal stories unfold, they run into <i>big</i> trouble with some older dudes, but rein victorious......or so they think.</p><p>In the end, the boys discover many tough, but important lessons about life, and the narrator <i>(unlike in the movie)</i> reveals how three of the lads sadly meet their untimely demise. The only downside for me is wishing I would have skipped the five plus hours of audio and opted for a good old ordinary book. 4.5 Stars.</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="15122196476138088919" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3515776104" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9054340579213089303">I really enjoyed this story. I've read other Stephen King short stories and I must confess: he has a way with words and storytelling. Sometimes human emotion is diluted by words and loses its effect. Stephen King however, delivers every time.<br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1598619722i/30027547._SX540_.png' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>The story was the inspiration for the 1987 film 'Stand By Me'. The story is a first-person narrative by the main character, Gordon LaChance. He is telling the story many years later as an adult. He reflects about himself and his three best friends during the</span>
  <span id="freeText9054340579213089303" style="display:none">I really enjoyed this story. I've read other Stephen King short stories and I must confess: he has a way with words and storytelling. Sometimes human emotion is diluted by words and loses its effect. Stephen King however, delivers every time.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1598619722i/30027547._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>The story was the inspiration for the 1987 film 'Stand By Me'. The story is a first-person narrative by the main character, Gordon LaChance. He is telling the story many years later as an adult. He reflects about himself and his three best friends during the summer of 1960. The four 12-year-olds are going on a journey to see a dead body. During their journey they experience the full spectrum of human emotion and come out of the journey a little different, a little more mature. <br><br>The readability and realism kept me engaged the entire time. I would highly recommend this alongside "Shawshank Redemption" because of the human element layered in the story. Stephen King is a great writer all around: horror and drama. Thanks!</span>

  <span id="freeText9580316796894167559" style="display:none">4.5 Stars<br /><br />I LOVE the movie Stand By Me. <br /><br />I don't know if its because its a great film(it is). Or if it's because I watched it around the same time, I watched movies like The Sandlot and My Girl. So in my mind I just equate them with summers in my childhood. <br /><br />I haven't watched Stand By Me in probably over 15years, it was probably around the same time I read this book for the first time. I remember not really liking the book back then. I think it was the combination of it being a non-horror Stephen King book and/or because I loved the movie so much. <br /><br />This time around I loved this book the only thing stopping it from being a 5 star read is that its in my opinion too short. I want more. As an adult I read this book in a completely different way and I'm sure that I would view the movie differently now as well. <br /><br />The Body is about 4 friends who decide to go on a weekend "adventure" to find the rumored body of a kid who was hit by a train. <br /><br />It's Stephen King so know its gonna be a darker read but despite that morbid premise The Body is a story of friendships. The boys are around 12 years old and they've been inseparable for years but they can feel themselves growing apart. High School tends to do that. When I was a kid I hung out with about 6 girls and we just knew we would be friends forever but by sophomore year I was only still friends with 3 and as an adult I'm only friends with 1. <br /><br />Times change. In high school I found my tribe but I still have fond memories of my preteen "girl gang", even if I'm no longer friends with them. <br /><br />The Body is the perfect gateway drug to start reading Stephen King. Its not scary, its not gory(maybe a little bit gory), and it has a lot of heart.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9580316796894167559" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText17572276676662594394" style="display:none">I decided that in 2020 I would try harder to read some Stephen King. After reading The Body I wish I had started with it. <br /><br />I didn’t really care for the two short stories within the novella. When the first one began I was wondering if I had a weird electronic version that mixed two separate novels together. <br /><br />What I liked most about the novella is the idea that the most important things are the hardest things to say. <br /><br />‘The most important things are the hardest things to say. They are things you get ashamed of, because words make them smaller. When they were in your head they were limitless; but when they come out they seem to be no bigger than normal things. But that's not all. The most important things lie too close to wherever your secret heart is buried; they are clues that could guide your enemies to a prize they would love to steal. It's hard and painful for you to talk about these things ... and then people just look at you strangely. They haven't understood what you've said at all, or why you almost cried while you were saying it’ <br /><br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6941945903174573774">This story is a journey of young boys growing up, loss of innocence, and friendship. This is probably the most authentic coming of age story I've experienced. These kids feel like REAL kids. They act and speak like actual 12 year old boys would, which I'm thankful for because I think a lot of adults who write about young kids or teens have forgotten what it's actually like to be one.<br/><br/>Stephen King can definitely write the best child characters. The amount of depth he installs in his characters and</span>
  <span id="freeText6941945903174573774" style="display:none">This story is a journey of young boys growing up, loss of innocence, and friendship. This is probably the most authentic coming of age story I've experienced. These kids feel like REAL kids. They act and speak like actual 12 year old boys would, which I'm thankful for because I think a lot of adults who write about young kids or teens have forgotten what it's actually like to be one.<br /><br />Stephen King can definitely write the best child characters. The amount of depth he installs in his characters and development of them is amazing. They feel real, the feel like actual people. Also, what great story telling! I flew through the pages without even noticing. I was totally immersed, and that's always a good sign.<br /><br />This story had funny moments, relatable ones and the final few pages, I read with tears in my eyes. Brilliant story, great unforgettable characters and what good writing! Perfect read! 5 glorious stars and more.</span>

The setting is 1960s Maine where four young teens take a walking adventure trip looking for a dead boy's body. If you grew up in the 50s and 60s, this is the kind of stuff you would do, damn the consequences. Great story telling by a master story teller.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2619848456" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8685491891077465094"><i>“The most important things are the hardest to say, because words diminish them. It’s hard to make strangers care about the good things in your life.”</i><br/><br/>Besides <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/239579.Charles_Dickens' title='Charles Dickens' rel='nofollow noopener'> Charles Dickens</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15305372.Ruth_Hogan' title='Ruth Hogan' rel='nofollow noopener'> Ruth Hogan</a>, Stephen King is one of the few authors I admire who has always been able to stir so many different emotions in me in a single book.<br/> <br/>Any other author could have written the simple story of 4 adolescents, who decide to embark on a journey to see the body of another youth, who has been killed by a train. But no</span>
  <span id="freeText8685491891077465094" style="display:none"><i>“The most important things are the hardest to say, because words diminish them. It’s hard to make strangers care about the good things in your life.”</i><br /><br />Besides <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/239579.Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens" rel="nofollow noopener"> Charles Dickens</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15305372.Ruth_Hogan" title="Ruth Hogan" rel="nofollow noopener"> Ruth Hogan</a>, Stephen King is one of the few authors I admire who has always been able to stir so many different emotions in me in a single book.<br /> <br />Any other author could have written the simple story of 4 adolescents, who decide to embark on a journey to see the body of another youth, who has been killed by a train. But not anyone could have conveyed the senses of pleasure, misery, horror, rage, decency, camaraderie, wonderment and sympathy embedded throughout the story.<br /></span>

        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/40275186-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/39339086-the-body">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40275186-sarah?shelf=horror">horror</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2449134300" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13003731050362401613">This was okay. I listened to it on audio, narrated by Frank Muller. I think this was my first time listening to him as a narrator, and I thought he was great. <br/><br/>The story itself was nothing special. It was very classic Stephen King, a coming of age story for four boys in Castle Rock, Maine. I was reminded a lot of IT- not because anything in the book was all that scary, but just because the boys seemed like they were doing a lot of the same things. (Walking train tracks through the woods, battling</span>
  <span id="freeText13003731050362401613" style="display:none">This was okay. I listened to it on audio, narrated by Frank Muller. I think this was my first time listening to him as a narrator, and I thought he was great. <br /><br />The story itself was nothing special. It was very classic Stephen King, a coming of age story for four boys in Castle Rock, Maine. I was reminded a lot of IT- not because anything in the book was all that scary, but just because the boys seemed like they were doing a lot of the same things. (Walking train tracks through the woods, battling bullies, swimming, etc.) One of them is even a writer- which I believe one of the kids in IT grows up to be?<br /><br />The whole thing was so similar it just struck me as odd. Sure, King reuses a lot of the same themes, (set in Maine, writers, coming of age, kids as heroes, bullies, etc.) but nothing I've ever read from him made me go- gee, haven't I heard this story before? It just seemed lazy to me.<br /><br />The highlight, for me, was the stories we're given from the writer, Gordie. He has one about a man named Chico, that's autobiographical for him in a way, and I really enjoyed it. In the end, I'm not really sure what the point of The Body was. Kids go on a walk to find a body. That's pretty much it. Maybe I just couldn't relate.<br /><br />I know this was originally part of a collection, so I wouldn't rule the entire collection out- I just wouldn't bother re-reading this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13003731050362401613" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3486558688" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3486558688" />
    <a title="Literature &amp; Lofi - Benjamin Blackmore" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/112967409-literature-lofi---benjamin-blackmore"><img alt="Literature &amp; Lofi - Benjamin Blackmore" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602335600p2/112967409.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3486558688?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Literature &amp; Lofi - Benjamin Blackmore" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Literature &amp; Lofi - Benjamin Blackmore" href="/user/show/112967409-literature-lofi---benjamin-blackmore">Literature &amp; Lofi - Benjamin Blackmore</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781982103538" href="/book/show/38926465-the-body">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/112967409-literature-lofi---benjamin-blackmore?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3486558688" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9901874216844680277"><b> "Some people drown, that’s all. It’s not fair, but it happens..." </b><br/><br/>My god! I expected to be impressed by this charming novella, but I never expected to fall in love with this tale as much as I did. A group of four tight-knit friends border lining the beginning of their teenage years agree to journey out of town to look at a dead body. <br/><br/>Stephen King captures the coming of age journey perfectly. We witness this adventure transform each individual so poignantly. Gone is the innocence and nativity to</span>
  <span id="freeText9901874216844680277" style="display:none"><b> "Some people drown, that’s all. It’s not fair, but it happens..." </b><br /><br />My god! I expected to be impressed by this charming novella, but I never expected to fall in love with this tale as much as I did. A group of four tight-knit friends border lining the beginning of their teenage years agree to journey out of town to look at a dead body. <br /><br />Stephen King captures the coming of age journey perfectly. We witness this adventure transform each individual so poignantly. Gone is the innocence and nativity to be replaced with something new. A fresh perspective on life. One that is dancing around dark thoughts of the macabre, a realism distinguishing optimism and undeniable truth. I particularly enjoy how the journey reveals every character's flaw with the perpetual realization that inevitably everything comes to an end.<br /><br />"Friends come and go like waiters in a restaurant, did you ever notice that?"<br /><br /><br />This is without a doubt the best exploration of friendship that I have seen in any piece of literature work. While perhaps not the best embodiment of friendship, we do exploration every single dynamic of how friendships work, how they thrive, and how they can drift apart. The antics our four characters get up are without a doubt relatable to any adult reminiscing on their teenage years. With the shadow of adulthood looming over them, we're truly indulged as to what specific moment made these boys become men.<br /><br />There are just so many running themes beautifully represented and packed cozily within the 179 pages. I particularly loved King's superb focus on social identities, reality versus idealism, and the pressures of adolescence to name a few.<br /><br />There are so many iconic scenes that will forever stick in my mind. Perhaps I should even mention a few refreshing lessons to take away from this story as well. There's something almost enlightening with this read and I am left descending a deep rabbit hole of nostalgia reflecting that everything has worked out exactly the way things should be.<br /><br /><b> Final verdict: 9.5 out of 10 </b></span>
  <a data-text-id="9901874216844680277" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3374185682" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3374185682" />
    <a title="Jaclyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10651001-jaclyn"><img alt="Jaclyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581215573p2/10651001.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3374185682?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jaclyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jaclyn" href="/user/show/10651001-jaclyn">Jaclyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10651001-jaclyn?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10651001-jaclyn?shelf=stephen-king">stephen-king</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10651001-jaclyn?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10651001-jaclyn?shelf=maine">maine</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10651001-jaclyn?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3374185682" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17400087585071079735">The ways in which kids feel pain, and love and loss are distinct. The Body is an adventure from the outset, a tale of friendship and a coming of age novella about the loss of four young boys innocence. Do the people you surround yourself with hold you up, or do they hold you back? Will they stand by you? This theme is questioned throughout the book, notably from the wiser than his years Chris Chambers. One of my favorite scenes in the book comes near the end, when the boys are making their stand</span>
  <span id="freeText17400087585071079735" style="display:none">The ways in which kids feel pain, and love and loss are distinct. The Body is an adventure from the outset, a tale of friendship and a coming of age novella about the loss of four young boys innocence. Do the people you surround yourself with hold you up, or do they hold you back? Will they stand by you? This theme is questioned throughout the book, notably from the wiser than his years Chris Chambers. One of my favorite scenes in the book comes near the end, when the boys are making their stand against Ace Merrill and his crew. Vern and Teddy abandon Chris, but Gordie stands with him. <br /><br /><i>"Stick with me, Gordie," Chris said in a low, shaky voice.<br />"stick with me, man."<br />"I'm right here."</i><br /><br />This scene solidifies Gordies decision to stick with Chris, because Gordie realizes without him, Chris will drown. This is followed through at the end of the book, when Gordie discusses studying with Chris for college, while Teddy &amp; Vern goof off and get high. This novella is as much about friendship as it is about growing up, and I love it for that. Friendship isn't always easy, and it doesn't always last. Friends are like seasons – they come and go. <br /><br />This is a poignant novella to read as an adult, and would be a wonderful introduction to SK for a younger audience, despite all the swearing. The themes here are important, relatable, and prominent. <br /><br />Connections:<br />Castle Rock Story<br />Chamberlaine, Maine is the town that Ray Brower is from - same town <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10592.Carrie" title="Carrie by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Carrie</a>
</i> is set in<br />Possible <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11588.The_Shining" title="The Shining by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Shining</a>
</i> reference pg 41 "A Gordon Lachance halfway along in the process of losing the shine" most likely indicating a loss of innocence, but interesting wording for constant readers<br />Bannerman - not present but discussed - Character in <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10603.Cujo" title="Cujo by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Cujo</a>
</i> &amp; <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11573.The_Dead_Zone" title="The Dead Zone by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dead Zone</a>
</i><br />Ace Merril - Pop Merrill's nephew. mentioned in <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/50913926.The_Sun_Dog" title="The Sun Dog by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sun Dog</a>
</i>, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/107291.Needful_Things" title="Needful Things by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Needful Things</a>
</i>, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27256336.Skeleton_Crew_Nona" title="Skeleton Crew Nona by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Skeleton Crew: Nona</a>
</i><br />Vern Tessio - <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27256336.Skeleton_Crew_Nona" title="Skeleton Crew Nona by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Skeleton Crew: Nona</a>
</i><br />Teddy Duchamp - Character w/ same name mentioned in <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10592.Carrie" title="Carrie by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Carrie</a>
</i>, as it is established in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11573.The_Dead_Zone" title="The Dead Zone by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dead Zone</a>, that Carrie is a fictional story with in the SK universe, possible that Gordon Lachance is the fictional writer of Carrie? A stretch but fun hypothesis <br />Emporium Galorium- owned by Pop Merrill <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/50913926.The_Sun_Dog" title="The Sun Dog by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sun Dog</a>
</i><br />Shawshank redemption mentioned (and coincidentally where Ace Merrill eventually ends up) <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/39664.Rita_Hayworth_and_Shawshank_Redemption_A_Story_from_Different_Seasons" title="Rita Hayworth and Shawshank Redemption A Story from Different Seasons by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Rita Hayworth and Shawshank Redemption: A Story from Different Seasons</a>
</i><br />Possible <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10628.Night_Shift" title="Night Shift by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Night Shift</a>
</i> reference on pg 75 "We knew about the night shift" when talking about a book of scary stories <br /><i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11573.The_Dead_Zone" title="The Dead Zone by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dead Zone</a>
</i> reference pg 110 "The Wheel of Fortune"<br />Mentions Derry, Jerusalem's Lot &amp; Lewiston - all locations used in SK's works<br />Aunt Evvie Chalmers - referenced in <i> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/50913926.The_Sun_Dog" title="The Sun Dog by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sun Dog</a></i> , <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10603.Cujo" title="Cujo by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Cujo</a>
</i>, <i>

<div id="review_152835798" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/152835798" />
    <a title="J.C." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4305144-j-c"><img alt="J.C." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535519062p2/4305144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/152835798?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.C." class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.C." href="/user/show/4305144-j-c">J.C.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780143143925" href="/book/show/4945935-the-body">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4305144-j-c?shelf=movie">movie</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer152835798" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4371663276135162405">I feel like I just watched an audio-only version of the film "Stand By Me". Which makes sense as this was the original story that inspired the movie. And its surprising how much the movie not only followed the original story, but captured much of its spirit. Certainly the King version is much more vulgar and up front, but the wonder of youth and the harsh reality of time is there, in the film. <br/><br/>I've always wanted to read this. I've always been a huge fan of the film and was always curious as to K</span>
  <span id="freeText4371663276135162405" style="display:none">I feel like I just watched an audio-only version of the film "Stand By Me". Which makes sense as this was the original story that inspired the movie. And its surprising how much the movie not only followed the original story, but captured much of its spirit. Certainly the King version is much more vulgar and up front, but the wonder of youth and the harsh reality of time is there, in the film. <br /><br />I've always wanted to read this. I've always been a huge fan of the film and was always curious as to King's take on it. Much of King's material goes back to a group of kids, a pack, going through some kind of bizarre adventure. One only has to look at "It" and "Dreamcatcher" to see this. Both are very strong tales, some say his best (I've only read Dreamcatcher, FYI), and that is the same case here. <br /><br /><br />The one thing I will say that seperates the tale from the film is the ending. It's much broader here, more real and more heart breaking. I wont spoil it beyond that point. But, even though I hate to see things turn out in such a fashion for those characters, it makes sense to me. I think it's logical given the theme of the story and I don't think it's harsh or cruel or out of context. <br /><br />If you enjoyed the movie or enjoy King's work this is another that I highly recommend. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4371663276135162405" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14798915862041722444"> A moving account of four twelve year olds in 1960 embarking on a ridiculous adventure that is beautifully written and incredibly moving. One of King's best: no ghosts, vampires, horror or supernatural things or beings (of which he is a master). Just four boys doing the kind of thing that 12 year old boys do. The very successful movie "Stand by Me" is based on this novella.</span>

<div id="review_1097609694" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1097609694" />
    <a title="Marie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13987856-marie"><img alt="Marie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1421307125p2/13987856.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1097609694?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marie" href="/user/show/13987856-marie">Marie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1097609694" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7787770169442197287">The straightforward, but somehow magical recount of four boys looking for a body, sounds like it should be so much less than it is. The harsh reality of life, leaking out of a would-be romantic childhood adventure, lulls you into the wonder of youthful friendship, then slams you hard with the realization that life happens to everyone, and nothing about that is fair. <br/>You wouldn't need to read between the lines for this to be a great story. King lays everything out for you, gut-wrenching but heart</span>
  <span id="freeText7787770169442197287" style="display:none">The straightforward, but somehow magical recount of four boys looking for a body, sounds like it should be so much less than it is. The harsh reality of life, leaking out of a would-be romantic childhood adventure, lulls you into the wonder of youthful friendship, then slams you hard with the realization that life happens to everyone, and nothing about that is fair. <br />You wouldn't need to read between the lines for this to be a great story. King lays everything out for you, gut-wrenching but heartfelt, take it or leave it. I've always liked it when a story had the power to make me cry, and this one did far more than that. Nothing that bad happens though, it isn't epic and romantic, not at all, but it's real and it feels real. It made me angry and sad, gasping for breath and tears rolling down my cheeks, slamming my head down into my pillow, at the unfairness of it all. I've cried over many a book before, but I have never felt quite like this before, satisfied, but snubbed. Any story that can illicit that response from me is all right in my book, and I give it my recommendation with the highest regard.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7787770169442197287" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText12646853295011343626" style="display:none">The only other thing that I've ever read by Stephen King roughly the first 200 pages of Under The Dome. I gave up on it after I realized how vulgar the writing was. That was a while ago, and I have read many books since then, and my tastes have developed more. I decide to try out reading The Body because it was classified as young adult, and it might be less... <br />The book was perfectly fine, It was almost similar to the Outsiders. It was just shy of 200 pages, and if it was any longer, I would have never entertained the idea of reading it. It was still a little graphic to be classified as young adult, but I suppose that is the best you will get with a Stephen King book.<br /> <br />3.5/5 stars. I may read something else by Stephen King, but it will not be anywhere in the foreseeable future.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1458481296" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9892510510599190284">The inspiration for the highly-rated 1986 film <i>Stand By Me</i>, <i>The Body</i> is different than Stephen King's usual writing. Unlike his gory horror novels, this short story is just a book about four misfit kids who go on a hike to find a rumored dead body downtown, but on the way, they learn about friendship and family and reputation, and they also learn the real meaning of death, and what once became an immature adventure they eventually see the seriousness behind.</span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6375091730842592095"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1571616007i/28326506._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='300' alt='description"/' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Reading this book reminded me of how much I love reading books that are either narrated by a children or feature children in them as the main characters. This was a great coming-of-age story. It made me smile, laugh and, feel sorry for the kids. It's a great story of friendship and childhood. This is my third Stephen King novella and something I've noticed is that all his books seem to have references from the other books. For example, in this book the fictional prison of Shawshank is mentioned.</span>
  <span id="freeText6375091730842592095" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1571616007i/28326506._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt='description"/' class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Reading this book reminded me of how much I love reading books that are either narrated by a children or feature children in them as the main characters. This was a great coming-of-age story. It made me smile, laugh and, feel sorry for the kids. It's a great story of friendship and childhood. This is my third Stephen King novella and something I've noticed is that all his books seem to have references from the other books. For example, in this book the fictional prison of Shawshank is mentioned. That was a reference to Rita Hayworth and the Shawshank Redemption. Really cool! All in all, it was a quick and fun read. Would recommend. <br><br>Going to check out the movie to this! <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1571615928ra/28326484.gif" width="400" height="300" alt='description"/' class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ppwRRne/tFcs+TGWJYRCb36Tph9y9X4T5Aa/xpOm7XnRT/h5EFOe9TsCqtyxdjuAeWRqO3bhG2L4qSeq0tomOA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('AJUbYWwXJJlUL9xuX0RKXepwOpHzp4j0txS1IGko9Hl3RvJeC/sOO0PURyTLtjOy7Yf2tfez7YWruy1MKFQ/OA==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3389.Stephen_King">About Stephen King</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/3389.Stephen_King"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1362814142p3/3389.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/3389.Stephen_King">Stephen King
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
750,330
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1999637231690756518">Stephen Edwin King was born the second son of Donald and Nellie Ruth Pillsbury King. After his father left them when Stephen was two, he and his older brother, David, were raised by his mother. Parts of his childhood were spent in Fort Wayne, Indiana, where his father's family was at the time, and in Stratford, Connecticut. When Stephen was eleven, his mother brought her children back to Durham, M</span>
  <span id="freeText1999637231690756518" style="display:none">Stephen Edwin King was born the second son of Donald and Nellie Ruth Pillsbury King. After his father left them when Stephen was two, he and his older brother, David, were raised by his mother. Parts of his childhood were spent in Fort Wayne, Indiana, where his father's family was at the time, and in Stratford, Connecticut. When Stephen was eleven, his mother brought her children back to Durham, Maine, for good. Her parents, Guy and Nellie Pillsbury, had become incapacitated with old age, and Ruth King was persuaded by her sisters to take over the physical care of them. Other family members provided a small house in Durham and financial support. After Stephen's grandparents passed away, Mrs. King found work in the kitchens of Pineland, a nearby residential facility for the mentally challenged.<br /><br />Stephen attended the grammar school in Durham and Lisbon Falls High School, graduating in 1966. From his sophomore year at the University of Maine at Orono, he wrote a weekly column for the school newspaper, THE MAINE CAMPUS. He was also active in student politics, serving as a member of the Student Senate. He came to support the anti-war movement on the Orono campus, arriving at his stance from a conservative view that the war in Vietnam was unconstitutional. He graduated in 1970, with a B.A. in English and qualified to teach on the high school level. A draft board examination immediately post-graduation found him 4-F on grounds of high blood pressure, limited vision, flat feet, and punctured eardrums.<br /><br />He met Tabitha Spruce in the stacks of the Fogler Library at the University, where they both worked as students; they married in January of 1971. As Stephen was unable to find placement as a teacher immediately, the Kings lived on his earnings as a laborer at an industrial laundry, and her student loan and savings, with an occasional boost from a short story sale to men's magazines.<br /><br />Stephen made his first professional short story sale ("The Glass Floor") to <i>Startling Mystery Stories</i> in 1967. Throughout the early years of his marriage, he continued to sell stories to men's magazines. Many were gathered into the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10628.Night_Shift" title="Night Shift" rel="nofollow noopener">Night Shift</a> collection or appeared in other anthologies.<br /><br />In the fall of 1971, Stephen began teaching English at Hampden Academy, the public high school in Hampden, Maine. Writing in the evenings and on the weekends, he continued to produce short stories and to work on novels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1999637231690756518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/2334601-the-body">Quotes from The Body</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;The most important things are the hardest things to say. They are things you get ashamed of, because words make them smaller. When they were in your head they were limitless; but when they come out they seem to be no bigger than normal things. But that's not all. The most important things lie too close to wherever your secret heart is buried; they are clues that could guide your enemies to a prize they would love to steal. It's hard and painful for you to talk about these things ... and then people just look at you strangely. They haven't understood what you've said at all, or why you almost cried while you were saying it.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
